Obligation of Placing Nose and Forehead in Sujood
Ruling on sujood requiring both nose and forehead, with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sujood regarding the nose and forehead, identify the hadith proof, and apply the exception for a valid excuse.

Core Idea
Both nose and forehead are wajib in sujood; only the nose suffices only with an excuse on the forehead.

This passage presents a concise ruling with proof on the obligatory body parts in sujood. It clarifies that in Hanafi fiqh, both the nose and forehead must be placed on the ground, and limiting to the nose alone invalidates the sujood unless a legitimate excuse prevents the forehead from touching. The ruling is grounded in the practice and command of the Prophet ﷺ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sujood (prostration) in salah. The ruling is that it is wajib to place both the nose and the forehead on the ground.
Wajib is a Hanafi category between fard and sunnah; omission requires compensation but does not invalidate the prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the hadith of Abu Humaid al-Sa'idi (Tirmidhi 270) describing the Prophet's sujood, as well as the general command to prostrate on seven bones (Bukhari, Muslim).
The dalil is zanni al-thubut (ahad), hence the ruling is wajib, not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'أمكن أنفه و جبهته' (he placed his nose and forehead) shows actual practice; the imperative 'أمر' in other narrations indicates obligation. Both parts are mentioned together, implying equal necessity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that only the forehead is fard and the nose is sunnah. This stems from a different reading of the same ahadith.
The Hanafi school strengthens its position with additional reports emphasizing the nose.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi within the school, supported by the zahir al-riwayah and the practice of the Companions.
The exception for an excuse on the forehead shows the school's pragmatism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسجد وجوبا بما يصلب من انفه و بجبهته ولا يصح الاقتصار على الانف الا من عذر بالجبهة
And he prostrates obligatorily with what is hard of his nose and his forehead, and it is not valid to limit it to the nose except due to an excuse on the forehead.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The author states that in sujood (prostration), it is wajib to place both the nose and the forehead on the ground. This is based on several hadith, including the practice of the Prophet ﷺ as narrated by Abu Humaid al-Sa'idi and the command to prostrate on seven bones. The ruling clarifies that prostrating only on the nose is invalid unless there is an excuse preventing the forehead from touching the ground. The Hanafi position is that both are obligatory (wajib); omission without excuse makes the prayer deficient but not invalid, requiring sajdah al-sahw if intentional.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub): A legal obligation between fard and sunnah; omission without excuse is sinful but not invalid unless from Zahir al-Riwayah.
اقتصار (iqtisar): Limiting sujood to only the nose without the forehead.
عذر (udhr): A legitimate excuse such as a wound or illness on the forehead.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling regarding sujood with only the nose touching the ground?
A) It is valid unconditionally.
B) It is invalid except when there is an excuse on the forehead.
C) It is makruh tahrimi.
D) It is valid only for women.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi salah, both nose and forehead are wajib in sujood; only the nose suffices only with a valid excuse on the forehead.

Height of Sujood Place Relative to Feet
Ruling on the permissible height difference for prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition regarding the height of the sujood place relative to the feet and identify the exception for crowding.

Core Idea
Sujood is invalid if the prostration spot is more than half a cubit above the feet, unless crowding forces prostration on a back.

In this lesson, we examine a specific condition for the validity of sujood in salah: the place where you rest your forehead should not be significantly higher than where you place your feet. The Hanafi school sets a precise limit of half a cubit (about 9 inches). Exceeding this invalidates the sujood unless there is extreme crowding, in which case Islam provides a practical solution: prostrating on the back of the worshipper in front. This ruling is based on transmitted evidence from the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its ruling
The passage states that the place of sujood must not be higher than the place of the feet by more than half a cubit. This is a condition (shart) for the validity of sujood. If exceeded, sujood is invalid (batil).
Half a cubit is approximately 9 inches.
2
Recognize the exception for crowding
An exception is made when there is crowding (zahma), e.g., in a packed mosque on Friday. In that case, one may prostrate on the back of the person in front, provided that person is also praying the same prayer.
This is a concession for necessity.
3
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The proof comes from two sources: (1) the practice of the Companions who prohibited the imam standing on a raised platform higher than the congregation, analogized to sujood; (2) the statement of 'Umar allowing prostration on a brother's back when unable to reach the ground due to crowding.
These are ahad hadith and qawl sahabi, giving zanni certainty.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of a high imam place is extended to sujood because both involve excessive elevation from the ground, breaking the prescribed posture. 'Umar's directive directly applies to the crowding scenario.
The analogy is based on the underlying cause (illa) of excessive height.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
While the classical Hanafi position holds to half a cubit, some later scholars considered up to one cubit permissible. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is half a cubit.
This is a minor ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدم ارتفاع محل السجود عن موضع القدمين باكثر من نصف ذراع وان زاد على نصف ذراع لم يجز السجود الا لزحمة سجد فيها على ظهر الانف
And the place of sujood not being higher than the place of the feet by more than half a cubit; and if it exceeds half a cubit, then sujood is not valid except due to crowding, then he prostrates on the back of the person in front.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding the level difference between the place of sujood and the feet. The condition is that the prostration spot should not be more than half a cubit (approx. 9 inches) higher than where the feet are placed. If it is higher, the sujood is invalid because it would involve excessive bending, deviating from the prescribed posture. The exception is in a crowded situation (e.g., in a mosque during Friday prayer) where one may prostrate on the back of the person in front, provided that person is also praying the same prayer. This is based on a hadith from Hudhayfa and the practice of Umar. The Hanafi position is that this is a condition for the validity of sujood.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A cubit, approximately 18 inches; half a dhira' is about 9 inches.
زحمة (zahma): Crowding, especially in congregational prayer where one cannot find space to prostrate normally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Sujood is not valid unless there is crowding.
C) Sujood is valid but makruh.
D) Sujood is invalid even in crowding.

Key Takeaway
The condition for valid sujood is that the forehead's resting place must not be more than half a cubit above the feet, except in crowded conditions where prostration on another's back is allowed.

Placing Hands and Knees on Ground in Sujood
Ruling on placing hands and knees on the ground during sujood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on placing hands and knees in sujood and explain that it is a sunnah practice based on a sahih riwaya.

Core Idea
Placing hands and knees on the ground in sujood is a confirmed sunnah (sahih riwaya), not an obligatory act.

This lesson addresses a specific element of the sujood posture: placing both hands and both knees on the ground. The text states that the correct narration (sahih riwaya) supports this practice. In the Hanafi school, this ruling is understood as a recommended sunnah, not a requirement for validity. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion between sunnah and wajib in salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is placing both hands and both knees on the ground during sujood. The text states it is based on a 'sahih riwaya' (correct narration).
This is a ruling statement; no other details are given.
2
Determine the hukm level
In Hanafi fiqh, a 'sahih riwaya' for a practice indicates it is a sunnah (recommended), not fard or wajib.
The term 'riwaya' suggests transmission, not legislative obligation.
3
Understand the implication for validity
Since it is sunnah, omitting it does not invalidate the prayer. There is no requirement for sajdah al-sahw.
This is a key distinction: sunnah acts are meritorious but not required.
4
Memorize the concise ruling
The ruling is: placing hands and knees in sujood is a sunnah act based on the authentic transmission in the Hanafi school.
This can be used in fatwa or personal practice.

The omission of this does not invalidate the prayer, but it is better to observe.

Obligatory Body Parts in Sujood: Hands, Knees, and Toes
Rulings on body parts in sujood with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi wajib rulings for hands, knees, and toes in sujood, and derive the obligation from the hadith of the seven bones.

Core Idea
In sujood, it is wajib to place the hands, knees, and some part of the toes on the ground, not the top of the foot.

In the Hanafi school, prostration (sajdah) has specific conditions regarding which body parts must touch the ground. This lesson examines the obligation of placing the hands, knees, and toes, based on a hadith from Ibn Abbas. Understanding this ruling ensures the validity of one's prayer according to the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that placing the hands and knees is 'in al-sahih' (the correct view), and that some part of the toes must be placed; the top of the foot is insufficient. Identify these as the Hanafi position.
The phrase 'في الصحيح' indicates the relied-upon position.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Ibn Abbas is cited: the Prophet ﷺ was commanded to prostrate on seven bones: forehead, hands, knees, and tips of the feet. This is the proof.
The hadith is in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze wajh al-dalalah
The command 'amirtu' (I was commanded) indicates obligation in Hanafi usul. The enumeration of the bones shows each must be placed. 'Atraf al-qadamayn' is interpreted as the toes.
Hanafi usul: al-amr li al-wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools consider these actions sunnah, but the Hanafi school holds them wajib due to the command in the hadith.
The difference stems from the interpretation of the imperative.
5
Apply to practice
In sujood, ensure that the palms, knees, and at least some part of the toes are firmly on the ground. The top of the foot alone is insufficient.
If any wajib is omitted, sajdah sahw is required if deliberate.

Precedence of Bowing Before Prostration
Ruling that ruku' must come before sujood in each rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order of ruku' and sujood, explain the evidence from the Sunnah, and distinguish between intentional and forgetful reversal.

Core Idea
Ruku' must precede sujood; reversing the order intentionally invalidates the rak'ah.

In the daily prayer, every rak'ah has a specific sequence of actions. One of the most fundamental is that bowing (ruku') must come before prostration (sujood). The Hanafi school considers this order obligatory (wajib), based on the practice of the Prophet. This lesson examines the evidence and explains the consequences of acting contrary to this sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing ruku' before sujood in each rak'ah. The Hanafi ruling is that this order is wajib (obligatory).
Wajib in Hanafi law means that omission without valid reason requires compensation (sajdah al-sahw) if forgetful, but if intentional, the act is invalid and must be repeated.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the hadith of Awf ibn Malik al-Ashja'i, who described the Prophet's prayer: he stood, bowed, then prostrated. This action demonstrates the prescribed sequence.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and is considered zanni al-thubut (probable transmission) and zanni al-dalalah (probable meaning).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The proof lies in the Prophet's consistent practice. In Hanafi usul, the action of the Prophet when done repeatedly and without exception indicates obligation (wujub).
The phrase 'ثم ركع ... ثم سجد' (then he bowed... then he prostrated) shows the sequence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
While the Hanafi school holds this order as wajib, some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider it sunnah (recommended). The difference arises from the interpretation of the Prophet's action.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that in the Shafi'i school, intentional reversal does not invalidate the prayer, while in the Hanafi school it does.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon because the Prophet's action is taken as a model for prayer, and the order is a consistent part of his practice. The evidence from the hadith is considered strong enough to establish obligation.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that ruku' must precede sujood; intentional reversal invalidates the rak'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقديم الركوع على السجود (٣٦٥) وجه : عن عوف بن مالك الاشجعي قال قمت مع رسول الله ﷺ ... ثم ركع بقدر قيامه يقول في ركوعه سبحان ذى الجبروت و الملكوت و الكبرياء و العظمة ثم سجد بقدر قيامه ثم قال في سجوده (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما یقول الرجل فی رکوعه و سجوده، ص ۱۳۵، نمبر ۸۷٣)
And giving precedence to bowing over prostration (365). Evidence: From Awf ibn Malik al-Ashja'i who said: I stood with the Messenger of Allah... then he bowed for the length of his standing, saying in his bowing: 'Glory be to the Possessor of power, dominion, magnificence and grandeur.' Then he prostrated for the length of his standing, then he said in his prostration... (Abu Dawud, no. 873)
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage establishes the order of ruku' before sujood as a required element. The Hanafi school holds that if one intentionally prostrates before bowing in a rak'ah, the prayer is not valid. The proof is from the action of the Prophet as narrated by Awf ibn Malik, showing the sequence. The verse mentioned in the Urdu (Surah al-Hajj, 22:77) also orders 'ruk'u wasjudu' with ruku' first.

Key Terms
تقديم الركوع على السجود (taqdim al-ruku' ala al-sujud): Performing ruku' before sujood in each rak'ah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The rak'ah is invalid and must be repeated
C) The prayer is invalid and must be restarted
D) Nothing; it is permissible

Key Takeaway
The order of ruku' before sujood is wajib; intentional reversal invalidates the rak'ah.

The Ruling on the Sitting Between the Two Prostrations
Ruling on the interval between the two prostrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of the sitting between the two prostrations and explain why it is wajib based on the evidence provided.

Core Idea
The sitting between the two prostrations is wajib; omission requires sajdah sahw.

This passage establishes two connected rulings: after the first prostration, one must raise to a near sitting (jalsah bayna al-sajdatayn), and then return to the second prostration. The Hanafi school emphasizes that this sitting is wajib, not merely sunnah, based on multiple hadith stressing the straightening of the back and the explicit command to sit. We will examine the evidence and the legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Pinpoint the act being ruled upon: raising from prostration to near sitting and then returning to prostration. Understand the ruling level: the passage states this is wajib according to the correct opinion.
The phrase 'على الأصح' indicates this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the first dalil
Read the hadith of Abu Mas'ud al-Badri: the prayer is not valid until the back is straightened in ruku' and sujud. Reflect on how this implies a pause and proper posture, including the sitting between prostrations.
This hadith establishes the general principle of stillness and straightening.
3
Examine the second and third dalil
Read the hadith of Anas and Abu Hurayrah. Anas describes the Prophet sitting until observers thought he had forgotten; Abu Hurayrah includes the explicit command 'then rise until you are at ease sitting.' Note the direct command indicating obligation.
The command 'ثم ارفع حتى تطمئن جالساً' is central to the Hanafi proof.
4
Determine the legal implication
Apply the Hanafi usul principle that a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub). The explicit command to sit between prostrations, combined with the Prophet's consistent practice, makes this act wajib.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufid al-wujub unless there is a qarinah (contextual evidence) to the contrary.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Recognize that the Shafi'i school considers the sitting between prostrations to be sunnah. Contrast their view based on interpreting the command as istihbab (recommendation). Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in the Hanafi school, omission requires sajdah sahw; in the Shafi'i school, no compensation is needed.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (legitimate difference of opinion) between the schools.

Source Text

(3) From Abu Hurayrah: ... then bow until you are at ease in bowing, then rise until you are standing straight, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then rise until you are at ease sitting, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then do that in all your prayer.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage establishes two connected rulings: first, after the first prostration, one must raise up to a sitting position near to the ground (qa'bah qareebah), and second, one must then return to the second prostration. The phrase 'ala al-ashah' indicates this is the correct position in the school. The evidence includes three hadith stressing the straightening of the back and the sitting between the two prostrations. The Hanafi school emphasizes that this sitting is wajib (obligatory), not merely sunnah.

Key Terms
قرب القعود (qurb al-qu'ud): Raising to a near-sitting position after prostration, not standing fully

Final Sitting for Tashahhud: Duration and Placement
Ruling on the final sitting and its placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the final sitting, explain its wajib status, and identify what constitutes its minimum duration and proper placement in prayer.

Core Idea
The final sitting is wajib and must be long enough for tashahhud, placed after all other pillars.

The final sitting (al-qu'ud al-akhir) is a critical moment in the prayer where the worshipper recites the tashahhud. But what is its legal weight? Is it a pillar (fard) or an obligation (wajib)? And when exactly must it occur? This lesson explores the Hanafi position, supported by hadith evidence, that the final sitting is wajib, not fard, and must be performed after all other pillars, with a duration sufficient to recite the tashahhud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what act is being discussed and classify its hukm level (wajib, fard, etc.) according to the Hanafi school.
The act is the final sitting (al-qu'ud al-akhir) for tashahhud.
2
Locate the Dalil
Identify the primary evidence cited: the hadith of Ibn Umar and the practice of the Companions before tashahhud was prescribed.
Two hadith are provided: (1) from Ibn Umar on completion of prayer after sitting; (2) from Ibn Mas'ud on the prescription of tashahhud.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how each hadith points to the ruling. The first shows that sitting is the final action; the second indicates that tashahhud was later made obligatory, so its place is at the end.
The first hadith: completion upon sitting before an event. The second: tashahhud was prescribed later, so its placement is final.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Observe that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider the final sitting a rukn (fard). The Hanafi classification as wajib affects the consequence of omission.
Cross-madhhab difference: Hanafi (wajib) vs. Shafi'i (fard).
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Understand that the Hanafi view is based on the strength of the evidence and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the school.
The hadith proofs are considered sufficient to establish wujub, not fardiyyah.

والقعود الاخير قدر التشهد وتأخيره عن الاركان (٣٦٧) وجه : (ا) عن ابن عمر ان رسول الله ﷺ قال اذا قضى الامام الصلوة وقعد فاحدث قبل ان يتكلم فقد تمت صلوته ومن كان خلفه ممن اتم الصلوة. (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الامام يحدث بعد ما یر فع راسه، ص ۱۰۰، نمبر ۱٦١٧ ترمذی شریف ، باب ماجاء فی الرجل يحدث بعد التشھد ، ص ۱۱۰ نمبر ٤٠٨) (2) عن ابن مسعود قال : كنا نقول قبل أن يفرض التشهد ، السلام على الله ، السلام علی جبرئیل و میکائیل الخ ۔ (سنن بيھقي، باب مبدأ فرض التشهد ، ج ثانی، ص ۱۹۸، نمبر ۲۸۱۹) (3) عن رفاعة بن رافع ان رسول الله ﷺ بينما هو جالس في المسجد يوما .
And the final sitting of the measure of tashahhud and its delay after the pillars (367). Evidence: (1) From Ibn Umar: The Prophet said, 'When the imam completes the prayer and sits, then he has an event (state of impurity) before speaking, his prayer is complete, and those behind him who completed the prayer.' (2) From Ibn Mas'ud: We used to say before tashahhud was prescribed: 'Peace be upon Allah, peace upon Jibra'il and Mika'il...' (3) From Rifa'ah ibn Rafi': While the Prophet was sitting in the mosque one day...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage covers two points: (a) the final sitting must be long enough to recite the tashahhud, and (b) this sitting must be the last of the prayer's pillars, meaning nothing else follows it except the closing (khuruj bi-sun'ihi). The first hadith shows that if the imam sits (for tashahhud) and then becomes impure, the prayer is complete, indicating the sitting (qa'dah) is the final prescribed action. The second hadith proves that tashahhud was later made obligatory, thus its place is at the end.

Key Terms
القعود الاخير (al-qu'ud al-akhir): The final sitting in prayer after the last prostration, during which tashahhud is recited

Key Takeaway
The final sitting in prayer is wajib, not fard; it must be performed after all other pillars and must be long enough to recite the tashahhud.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Final Sitting (Qa'dah Akhirah) is a Fard Pillar
Establishes final sitting as a pillar with its timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the final sitting, explain its evidence from the hadith of Ibn Umar, and apply the ruling to scenarios of omission.

Core Idea
The final sitting is a fard pillar — prayer is incomplete without it.

The final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) is a critical component of prayer. Many students assume it is merely a sunnah or that the tashahhud itself is the pillar. This lesson establishes from core Hanafi texts that the sitting itself is a fard pillar, supported by explicit hadith. Understanding this distinguishes between valid and invalid prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah). According to the passage, it is a fard (pillar) — not merely wajib or sunnah. The two points: it must be to the measure of tashahhud, and it must be performed after all other pillars.
Fard means omission intentionally invalidates the prayer.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The strongest evidence is the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'When the imam completes the prayer and sits, then has an invalidator before speaking, his prayer is complete.' This is cited from Sunan Abi Dawud and Tirmidhi.
The key phrase is 'wa qa'ada' (and sat).
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates that the prayer is considered complete only after sitting. The act of sitting itself is integral to completion, not merely the tashahhud recitation. Thus, the sitting is a necessary part (rukn).
Hanafi usul: the explicit mention of completion upon sitting implies obligation.
4
Note the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The Hanafi position is that the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud is fard. If omitted intentionally, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated. If omitted forgetfully, sujud al-sahw is required.
This is consistent with standard Hanafi fatwa.
5
Contrast with other views
Some early scholars considered the final sitting sunnah, but the Hanafi school follows the explicit dalil. The hadith's clear implication overrides weaker analogies.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is also consider it a rukn but require tashahhud as part of the sitting.

ترجمه : (۳۶۷) [۲۴] تشہد کی مقدار قعدہ اخیرہ کرنا ۔ [۲۵] اور قعدہ اخیرہ کو سب ارکان سے اخیر میں ادا کرنا۔ تشريح : یہاں دو باتیں ہیں [1] ایک بات یہ ہے کہ تشہد کی مقدار قعدہ اخیرہ کرے، [۲] اور دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ قعدہ اخیرہ سب ارکان کے بعد میں کرے، اس کے بعد ارکان میں سے صرف خروج بصنعہ باقی رہ جاتا ہے۔ وجه : (ا) عن ابن عمر ان رسول الله الله قال اذا قضى الامام الصلوة وقعد فاحدث قبل ان يتكلم فقد تمت صلوته ومن كان خلفه ممن اتم الصلوة. صل الله
(367) And the final sitting [must be] the measure of tashahhud and its delay from the pillars... [Urdu explanation followed by hadith proofs]. Translation: [24] Perform the final sitting to the measure of tashahhud. [25] And perform the final sitting after all the pillars. Commentary: Two matters: [1] The final sitting should be the measure of tashahhud, [2] And the final sitting should be after all pillars, after which only the exit by intent remains. Proof: Hadith of Ibn Umar: When the imam completes the prayer and sits, then has an invalidator before speaking, his prayer is complete... etc.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) in prayer. The author presents two key points: first, that the final sitting must be performed for at least the duration of the tashahhud recitation; second, that it must be performed after all other pillars of the prayer, leaving only the formal exit (khuruj bi-sun'ihi). The author then provides multiple hadith proofs. The strongest evidence for the obligatory nature of the final sitting comes from the hadith of Ibn Umar, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) states that if an imam completes the prayer and sits, then has an invalidating act before speaking, his prayer is complete. This indicates that the sitting itself is a fundamental part (fard) of the prayer, not merely a sunnah. The other hadith (from Ibn Mas'ud and Rifa'ah) further support that the tashahhud was later made obligatory, and that the final sitting completes the prayer. Thus, according to the Hanafi school, the final sitting is a fard pillar, and omitting it intentionally invalidates the prayer. If one omits it forgetfully, sujud al-sahw is required.

Key Terms
قعدہ اخیرہ (qa'dah akhirah): The final sitting in prayer after the last prostration, in which the tashahhud is recited.
تشہد (tashahhud): The specific supplication recited while sitting in prayer, beginning with 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) to the measure of tashahhud is a fard pillar; its intentional omission invalidates the prayer.

Performing Prayer Pillars While Awake
Mandates performing all pillars of prayer in a state of wakefulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that prayer pillars must be performed while awake and apply it to cases of drowsiness versus deep sleep.

Core Idea
A pillar performed in deep sleep is invalid; drowsiness is excused but wakefulness is required for valid prayer.

This lesson examines the Hanafi requirement that all pillars of prayer be performed in a state of wakefulness. The author provides a clear ruling and supports it with two hadith from Sahih Muslim, demonstrating the importance of conscious awareness in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing the pillars (arkan) of prayer. The ruling is that they must be performed while awake (wajib).
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited: from Aisha and from Abu Hurairah. Both indicate the need for awareness during prayer.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith of Aisha commands the drowsy person to sleep first, implying that prayer should be performed with full consciousness. The hadith of Abu Hurairah advises to lie down when recitation becomes difficult, further emphasizing awareness.
The command 'flarqad' (let him sleep) indicates that prayer in a drowsy state is improper.
4
Note the exception
Light drowsiness (nawm) is excused; no harm. But deep sleep (naum) during a pillar invalidates that pillar and it must be repeated.
The commentary explicitly differentiates between drowsiness and deep sleep.
5
Derive the practical application
If one performs a pillar in deep sleep, the pillar is invalid; repeat it. The rest of the prayer is valid if other conditions are met.
The entire prayer does not become invalid, only the specific pillar.

(368) And perform it [the pillars] while awake. Translation: [26] Perform all the pillars in a state of wakefulness. Commentary: If one performs pillars while asleep, it is possible that one intends to recite one thing and recites another, then the prayer becomes invalid. So he should perform all pillars while awake. If drowsiness overcomes, there is no harm. But if he performs a pillar while completely asleep, that pillar is not performed; he should repeat it. Proof: (1) From Aisha that the Prophet said: When one of you feels drowsy in prayer, let him sleep until the drowsiness goes away, because if he prays while drowsy, perhaps he intends to seek forgiveness but curses himself. This hadith indicates to pray while awake. (2) From Abu Hurairah... when one of you gets up at night and the Quran becomes difficult on his tongue, let him lie down. This hadith indicates that he should know what he is reciting.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the requirement to perform the prayer pillars (arkan) while in a state of wakefulness. The author explains that performing a pillar while asleep may lead to errors in recitation, invalidating the prayer. Light drowsiness is excused, but if one is fully asleep during a pillar, that pillar must be repeated. Two hadith are cited: from Aisha, advising to sleep off drowsiness before praying; and from Abu Hurairah, advising to lie down if recitation becomes unclear. These show that the prayer should be performed with full awareness. Thus, it is necessary to ensure wakefulness for each pillar.

Key Terms
ارکان (arkan): The essential pillars of prayer (e.g., qiyam, ruku', sujud), without which the prayer is invalid.
بیداری (bedari): A state of wakefulness and awareness, contrasted with sleep or drowsiness.
نعس/نوم (nu'as/nawm): Drowsiness (nu'as) is a state of light sleepiness; deep sleep (nawm) is full loss of consciousness.

Knowledge of Obligatory Acts in Prayer
Ruling on the obligation to know fard and sunnah in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that knowledge of fard and sunnah in prayer is wajib, and apply the intention-based distinction that performing a fard with nafl intention invalidates the fard.

Core Idea
Correct intention separates fard from nafl – misintention invalidates the obligatory act.

The validity of prayer depends on performing its obligatory pillars with the correct intention. This lesson explains why it is necessary to learn which acts are fard and which are sunnah, and what happens if the intention is mismatched. The author provides a proof from the hadith of teaching children prayer, implying the obligation of knowledge for adults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Determine the legal status of knowing the fard and sunnah acts in prayer. The passage states it is necessary (wajib) to distinguish them.
The ruling is derived from the hadith commanding teaching children; adults are analogously required.
2
Understand the Consequence of Misintention
Recognise that performing a fard act with the intention of a nafl (supererogatory) act invalidates that pillar. The example of Zuhr prayer is given.
Intention (niyyah) is a condition for the specific category of prayer.
3
Examine the Reverse Case
Note that performing a sunnah with the intention of fard still counts as sunnah, not fard. The intention does not upgrade the act.
This asymmetry shows intention only affects the intended category, not the objective nature of the act.
4
Analyse the Proof (Dalil)
The hadith of 'Teach the child prayer at seven and discipline at ten' implies that knowledge of prayer rules is essential. The command to teach obligates the teacher to know the rules first.
The dalil is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut), so the ruling is wajib, not fard.
5
Conclude the Practical Implication
A Muslim must learn the fard acts of prayer to avoid invalidating worship. Ignorance is not an excuse once one reaches maturity.
This ruling is agreed upon by all madhahib (muttafaq alayh).

(369) And knowledge of the manner of prayer and what is in it of obligatory characteristics, in a way that distinguishes them from the sunnah characteristics, or belief that they are fard, so that one does not perform a fard as a nafl. (370) And the pillars: then be straight in prostration, then sit and be calm sitting, then stand... Translation: [27] Knowledge of the manner of prayer and the obligatory characteristics in it, such that one can separate them from the sunnah, and have certainty that they are fard, so that one does not perform a fard as a supererogatory act. Commentary: The one praying should know how many sunnan and how many farā'id there are, and which are fard, so that he can distinguish each, so that he does not perform a fard thinking it is nafl. Because if he performs a fard thinking it is nafl, that pillar is not performed. However, if he performs a nafl thinking it is fard, it will count as nafl. For example, if one performs the fard of Zuhr with the intention of nafl, the fard is not performed; but if he performs the sunnah of Zuhr with the intention of fard and then repeats the fard, the sunnah will be valid as nafl. Proof: Hadith of Abd al-Malik... Teach the child prayer at seven...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the obligation to know the difference between the obligatory (fard) and recommended (sunnah) parts of prayer. The author emphasizes that a person must have knowledge of which acts are fard and which are sunnah, so that they do not inadvertently perform a fard with the intention of a nafl (supererogatory) act, which would render that pillar invalid. The commentary gives the example: if one performs the fard of Zuhr thinking it is a nafl, the fard does not count. Conversely, performing a sunnah with the intention of fard still counts as sunnah. The proof is a hadith urging parents to teach children the rules of prayer from age seven, implying that knowledge is essential for valid worship. Thus, this ruling obligates learning the fiqh of prayer.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligatory act commanded by definitive evidence; omission invalidates prayer.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory or voluntary prayer; performing it with the intention of fard does not count as fard but as nafl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person performs the fard of Zuhr prayer with the intention of performing a nafl (supererogatory) prayer?
A) The fard is performed and counts, because the action is the same.
B) The fard is not performed, and he must repeat it.
C) It is valid as a nafl prayer instead.
D) Both fard and nafl are invalid.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the fard and sunnah acts of prayer is wajib, and correct intention is crucial: performing a fard with nafl intention invalidates the fard.

Performing Prayer Pillars While Awake
Ruling and proof for performing pillars while awake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing prayer pillars in a state of sleep and distinguish between drowsiness and deep sleep.

Core Idea
A pillar performed in deep sleep is not valid and must be repeated while awake.

This lesson addresses a common situation: what if a worshipper performs a pillar of prayer while asleep? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on prophetic guidance. We will examine the proof and the precise condition of wakefulness required for valid prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that all pillars (arkan) of prayer must be performed while awake. Determine the hukm level: is it fard, wajib, or something else?
The text uses 'wajib' in the analytical toolbox, but the source itself implies necessity.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadiths from Muslim. Identify the authority and the specific commands.
Both are ahad hadiths, so thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do these hadiths prove that wakefulness is required? The first commands sleeping until drowsiness passes; the second commands lying down when recitation becomes unclear. Both indicate that prayer requires conscious awareness.
4
Distinguish between drowsiness and deep sleep
The passage clarifies that mere drowsiness (not overpowering) does not invalidate, but performing a pillar in deep sleep renders that pillar invalid and must be repeated.
This is a key nuance often misunderstood.
5
Note the practical consequence
If a pillar is missed due to sleep, the prayer is not automatically invalid; the missing pillar must be repeated while awake. If not repeated, the prayer becomes batil.
This differs from omitting a pillar intentionally, which invalidates immediately.

(368) And performing them while awake. (369) And knowing the manner of prayer and what obligatory traits are in it, in a way that distinguishes them from the sunnah traits, or believing that it is fard so that he does not perform a fard as a supererogatory act. (370) And the pillars: from 'then be moderate prostrating, then sit with tranquility sitting, then stand, and when you do that, your prayer is complete; and if you omit something from it, it is omitted from your prayer.' (Tirmidhi Sharif, chapter on description of prayer, p. 81, no. 302). In this hadith, it is that at the end he sat, so prayer is complete, from which it is known that sitting to the extent of tashahhud is fard.
Translation: (368) [26] Perform all pillars while awake.
Explanation: If he performs the pillars while sleeping, it is possible that he intends to recite some verse and recites something else, then the prayer becomes invalid. For this reason, all pillars must be performed while awake. If drowsiness overcomes him, there is no harm. But if he performs any pillar in deep sleep, that pillar is not performed; he must repeat that pillar.
Proof: (a) From Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you becomes sleepy in prayer, let him sleep until the sleep leaves him, for if one of you prays while drowsy, perhaps he goes to seek forgiveness and curses himself.' (Muslim Sharif, chapter on the command regarding one who is drowsy in his prayer, etc., p. 319, no. 1837/786). In this hadith, it is that he should pray while awake. (2) Abu Hurairah narrated from Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, and mentioned hadiths, including: And the Messenger of Allah said: 'When one of you stands for night prayer and the Quran becomes difficult on his tongue and he does not know what he is saying, let him lie down.' (Muslim Sharif, chapter on the command regarding one who is drowsy in his prayer, etc., p. 1319, no. 1836/787). In this hadith, it is that he should know what he is reciting.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the performance of prayer pillars while awake. The author begins by stating the requirement: all pillars (arkan) of prayer must be performed in a state of wakefulness. The term 'mutaqazzan' means awake and conscious. If one performs a pillar while completely asleep, that pillar is considered not performed and must be repeated. Mere drowsiness (ghunudighah) that does not overpower consciousness does not harm, but deep sleep invalidates the performance. The author then brings two hadith proofs: first, the hadith from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded one who becomes sleepy in prayer to sleep until the drowsiness leaves, because a drowsy person may inadvertently curse himself while intending to seek forgiveness. Second, the hadith from Abu Hurairah concerning night prayer: if the Quran becomes unclear and one does not know what he is saying, he should lie down. These indicate that prayer requires consciousness and awareness. The ruling is clear: all pillars, including recitation, must be performed intentionally and with awareness. The Hanafi school emphasizes this as a condition for validity.

Key Terms
بیداری (bidari): State of being awake and conscious, as opposed to sleep or overpowering drowsiness.
رکن (rukn): An integral part of prayer whose omission invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The ruku' is valid and prayer continues.
B) The ruku' is not valid and must be repeated when awake.
C) The entire prayer is invalid and must be restarted.
D) The prayer is valid if the person intended the ruku' before sleeping.

Key Takeaway
Prayer pillars require wakefulness; deep sleep invalidates the performance, but drowsiness does not.

Knowing Obligatory vs Sunnah in Prayer
Ruling on knowing fard vs sunnah in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on knowing fard and sunnah in prayer, explain the consequence of mistaken intention, and apply the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A fard act performed with nafl intention does not fulfill the obligation; knowing fard vs sunnah is wajib.

In this passage, the author emphasises that worshipers must know the obligatory and recommended parts of prayer to avoid misperformance. The ruling is anchored in a hadith commanding parents to teach children prayer, which implies teaching the distinction between fard and sunnah. Understanding this principle protects the validity of one's prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The act is the obligation to have knowledge of the fard and sunnah acts in prayer. The ruling concerns the worshiper's duty to distinguish them.
2
State the hukm level
Determine whether this knowledge is fard, wajib, sunnah, etc. According to the Hanafi school, it is wajib (necessary) not fard, because the proof is zanni.
Fard is from qati evidence; wajib from zanni evidence.
3
Extract the legal consequence
Identify what happens if a fard act is performed with the intention of nafl. The author states that the fard is not fulfilled; the act counts as nafl. Similarly, if a nafl is performed with fard intention, it still counts as nafl.
Intention (niyyah) determines the nature of the prayer, but the actual obligatory status of the act is also decisive.
4
Analyse the proof
The hadith commands teaching children prayer. The author uses this to infer that adults must also know the obligatory parts. The command implies teaching the distinction between fard and sunnah.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), hence the ruling is wajib not fard.
5
Consider the scope
This obligation applies to every accountable person (mukallaf) who prays. The ruling is particularly important for new Muslims or those learning prayer.
The hadith mentions children, but by analogy the obligation extends to all.

Source Text

Translation: (369) [27] The manner of prayer and the obligatory traits in it should be known in such a way that he can distinguish them from the sunnah traits, and he should be certain that it is fard so that he does not perform a fard considering it as a virtue (sunnah).
Explanation: The worshiper should know how many sunnah acts and how many fard acts are in prayer, and which are the fard acts, so that he can distinguish each, so that he does not perform a fard thinking it is a virtue (nafl). Because if he performs a fard thinking it is nafl, that pillar will not be performed. It is necessary that if he performs a nafl thinking it is fard, then the nafl will be performed. For example, if he performs the fard of Zuhr with the intention of nafl, the fard is not performed. But if he performs the sunnah of Zuhr with the intention of fard, and then later prays the fard again, the sunnah of Zuhr will be performed (as nafl).
Proof: 'Abdul Malik ibn al-Rabi' ibn Sabrah from his father from his grandfather said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Teach the boy prayer at seven years, and beat him for it at ten.' (Tirmidhi Sharif, chapter on what is mentioned about commanding children for prayer, p. 109, no. 407). In this hadith, it is that children should be taught the matters of prayer.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the obligation of knowing the fard and sunnah elements of prayer. The author emphasizes that a worshiper must have knowledge of which acts in prayer are obligatory (fard) and which are recommended (sunnah) so that he does not mistakenly perform a fard intending it as a supererogatory act (nafl). If a fard is performed with the intention of nafl, it does not count as the fard; the pillar is not considered performed. Conversely, if a sunnah is performed with the intention of fard, it counts as the sunnah (since the intention is for the sunnah, but if he later repeats the fard, the first prayer was valid as a nafl). The author cites the hadith commanding parents to teach children prayer at age seven and discipline at ten, indicating the importance of correct knowledge of prayer. This ruling underscores the need for religious education: one must know the obligatory elements to fulfill the prayer properly.

Key Terms
خصال مفروضة (khasal mafrudah): The obligatory traits or acts in prayer that are fard.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer, not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because the action is the same.
B) No, because the intention was for nafl, so the fard is not performed.
C) Yes, but he must repeat it as nafl later.
D) No, but the prayer counts as a valid nafl.

Key Takeaway
Knowing the fard and sunnah of prayer is wajib, and performing a fard with nafl intention does not fulfill the obligation.

The Pillars of Prayer and the Dispute on Final Sitting
Enumeration of prayer pillars and intra-Hanafi dispute on final sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the agreed-upon pillars of prayer and the disputed status of the final sitting, and explain the practical difference in rulings when the final sitting is omitted.

Core Idea
The final sitting is wajib, not rukn – omission requires compensation but does not invalidate.

This passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates the four pillars of prayer but notes that some Hanafi scholars, including the author of Hidayah, consider the final sitting to be a fard. Understanding this intra-Hanafi dispute is crucial for determining the ruling when the final sitting is omitted – whether the prayer becomes invalid or merely requires prostration of forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Agreed Pillars
Read the base text and list the four pillars explicitly mentioned: qiyam, qira'ah, ruku', sujud.
These are universally accepted by Hanafi scholars.
2
Note the Disputed Element
Recognize that the passage adds: 'Wa qila al-qu'ud al-akhir miqdar al-tashahhud' indicating a difference of opinion.
The final sitting is the subject of dispute.
3
Compare the Two Views
Identify the view of Hidayah (final sitting is fard) and the view of Nur al-Idah (only four pillars).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence
Work out the thamarah al-khilaf: if final sitting is fard, its omission invalidates prayer; if wajib, omission requires sajdah al-sahw but prayer is valid.
This is the key operational difference.
5
Learn the Mufta Bihi
Understand that the relied-upon position is that final sitting is fard (or at least wajib), so omission requires sajdah al-sahw or repetition depending on intentionality.
Most later Hanafi scholars follow this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذكورات اربعة القيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود . وقيل القعود الاخير مقدار التشهد ( ۳۷۱) وباقيها شرائط بعضها شرط لصحة الشروع في الصلوة وهو ما كان خارجها وغيره شرط لدوام صحتها .
ترجمه : (۳۷۰) ان تمام امور مذکورہ میں ارکان صرف چار ہیں:
ا قیام۔
قرآت۔
..... رکوع۔
م سجود
[۵] اور بعض حضرات نے تشہد کی مقدار قعدہ آخیرہ کو بھی فرض میں شمار کیا ہے
تشریح : صاحب ہدایہ نے نماز کے ارکان 4 شمار کئے ہیں، انہوں نے تکبیر تحریمہ کو بھی فرض میں شمار کیا ہے۔ ہدایہ کی عبارت یہ ہے۔ فرائض الصلوة ستة [١] التحريمة [۲] و القيام [۳] و القرأة [۴] و الركوع [۵] و السجود و [۶] القعدة آخيرة مقدار التشهد (ہدایہ اول، باب صفۃ الصلوۃ ، ص ۱۹۸، نمبر ۲۵۲) صاحب نور الایضاح نے تکبیر تحریمہ کو نماز سے پہلے [ باہر] شرائط میں شمار کیا ہے۔ اسی طرح صاحب ہدایہ نے قعدہ اخیرہ کو فرض کہا ہے، اور صاحب نور الایضاح نے فرمایا کہ یہ بعض حضرات کا قول ہے، اس لئے صاحب نورالایضاح کے یہاں فرض صرف ۴ رہ گئے
The mentioned (things) are four: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. And it is said: the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud. (371) And the rest are conditions; some are condition for the validity of beginning prayer, which is what is outside it, and others are condition for the continuation of its validity.
Translation: (370) Among all these mentioned matters, the pillars are only four:
1. Standing
2. Recitation
3. Bowing
4. Prostration
[5] And some respected scholars have also counted the final sitting to the extent of tashahhud as fard.
Explanation: The author of Hidayah has counted the pillars of prayer as four? Actually six: he has also included takbir tahrimah and final sitting. The text of Hidayah is: 'The fards of prayer are six: Tahrimah, Standing, Recitation, Bowing, Prostration, and Final Sitting to the extent of tashahhud.' (Hidayah vol.1, Bab sifat al-salah, p.198, no.252). The author of Nur al-Idah has counted takbir tahrimah among the conditions before prayer. Similarly, the author of Hidayah said that final sitting is fard, while the author of Nur al-Idah stated that this is the saying of some scholars, so according to Nur al-Idah, the fards remain only four.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise that identifies the pillars (arkan) of prayer and highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute. The base text (likely Nur al-Idah) states that the pillars are four: qiyam (standing), qira'ah (recitation), ruku' (bowing), and sujud (prostration). It then notes that some scholars (like the author of al-Hidayah) add the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) to the extent of tashahhud as a pillar. The commentary explains that according to al-Hidayah, the fards of prayer are six: takbir tahrimah, qiyam, qira'ah, ruku', sujud, and qa'dah akhirah. However, the author of Nur al-Idah considers takbir tahrimah as a condition outside prayer and considers the final sitting as only a saying of some scholars, thus maintaining that the essential pillars are four. This is a classic intra-Hanafi difference: the Hidayah represents a view that includes final sitting as fard, while Nur al-Idah exemplifies a stricter distinction between pillars and conditions. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school is that the final sitting is fard (wajib), though some later scholars treat it as a pillar. The passage helps students parse the technical vocabulary: 'arkan' (pillars) versus 'shurut' (conditions).

Key Terms
رکن (rukn): An integral part of prayer; if omitted intentionally or forgetfully, the prayer is invalid without repeating that pillar.
قعدہ اخیرہ (qa'dah akhirah): The final sitting in prayer after completing the second sajdah of the last rak'ah, to the extent of reciting tashahhud.
تشهد (tashahhud): The recitation of 'at-tahiyyat' during the sitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of Nur al-Idah, how many pillars (arkan) are there in prayer?
A) Four: standing, recitation, bowing, prostration
B) Five: adding takbir tahrimah
C) Six: adding takbir tahrimah and final sitting
D) Three: excluding recitation

Key Takeaway
The four agreed pillars of prayer are standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration; the final sitting is a disputed element within the Hanafi school, and the mufta bihi treats it as fard (wajib).

External and Internal Conditions of Prayer
Classification of non-pillar elements into two types of conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the non-pillar elements of salah into external conditions (for starting) and internal conditions (for continuation of validity) and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Prayer's non-pillar elements are conditions: some external for beginning, some internal for remaining valid.

The previous lesson covered the four pillars (arkan) of salah. This passage classifies everything else—the wajibat, sunan, and other requirements—as conditions (shurut). The author divides them into two categories: those that must exist before starting prayer (external) and those that must be maintained during prayer (internal). Understanding this classification helps you organize the components of salah and recognize why missing some elements requires compensation while missing others invalidates the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
The passage states that besides the four pillars, all remaining elements are conditions (shurut). Note the twofold division: conditions for beginning (external) and conditions for continuation (internal).
The four pillars are rukn; everything else is shart in this author's usage.
2
Distinguish external conditions
External conditions are those that must be present before starting prayer: purity (taharah), clean clothing, clean place. These are prerequisites for the validity of the beginning.
These are also known as shurut al-salah (conditions of prayer) in other texts.
3
Understand internal conditions
Internal conditions are those that must be maintained during prayer for its validity to continue, such as prostrating on both nose and forehead, and other wajibat and sunan.
The author includes wajibat and sunan under 'shart' for didactic purposes.
4
Recognize author's broad use of 'shart'
The author uses 'shart' broadly to include not only what other scholars call conditions but also the wajibat and sunan. This is a terminological feature of Nur al-Idah.
Other Hanafi texts may restrict 'shart' to only those elements whose absence invalidates the prayer entirely.
5
Relate to upcoming details
The passage signals that details of wajibat and sunan will follow, so this is a framework to categorize them.
Keep this classification in mind as you learn the specifics later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباقيها شرائط بعضها شرط لصحة الشروع في الصلوة وهو ما كان خارجها وغيره شرط لدوام صحتها .
ترجمه : (۳۷۱) ان کے علاوہ باقی تمام شرائط ہیں۔ ان میں سے بعض نماز کی صحیح ہونے کی شرطیں ہیں، اور یہ وہ ہیں جو نماز سے باہر ہیں ۔ اور اس کے علاوہ نماز صحیح ہو اور باقی رہے اس کی شرطیں ہیں ۔
تشریح : مصنف نے یہاں نماز کے اندر کے واجبات اور سنن کو شرط کہا ہے، اور نماز سے پہلے جو فرائض ہیں انکو بھی شرط کہا ہے۔ ۔ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ، چار فرضوں کے علاوہ سب نماز کی شرطیں ہیں، کچھ شرطیں وہ ہیں جو نماز سے باہر ہیں اور پہلے ہیں ، جیسے طہارت ہو، کپڑا پاک ہوجگہ پاک ہو۔ اور کچھ شرطیں وہ ہیں جو نماز کے اندر ہیں، جیسے ناک اور پیشانی دونوں پر سجدہ کرے وغیرہ واجبات اور سنن کی تفصیل آگے آرہی ہے۔
And the rest are conditions; some are condition for the validity of beginning prayer, which is what is outside it, and others are condition for the continuation of its validity.
Translation: (371) All the rest besides these are conditions. Among them some are conditions for the prayer being correct, and those are what are outside the prayer. And besides that, there are conditions for the prayer to be correct and to remain.
Explanation: The author has here called the internal wajibat and sunan as conditions, and also called the fard acts before prayer as conditions. The author says: besides the four fards, everything is a condition of prayer. Some conditions are those that are outside the prayer and come before, such as purification, clean clothing, clean place. And some conditions are those that are inside the prayer, such as prostrating on both nose and forehead, etc. The details of wajibat and sunan are coming ahead.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that classifies the remaining elements of prayer (other than the four pillars) as conditions (shurut). The author divides these conditions into two types: (1) conditions for the validity of beginning prayer (shurut li sihhat al-shuru'), which are external to the prayer, such as purity of body, clothing, and place; and (2) conditions for the continuation of validity (shurut li dawam sihhatiha), which are internal to the prayer, such as the manner of prostrating (on nose and forehead) and other wajibat and sunan. The author (Nur al-Idah) uses the term 'shart' broadly to include not only what other scholars call conditions but also the wajibat and sunan of prayer. This is a distinctive terminological choice: in Hanafi usul, a 'shart' is something that must exist for the act to be valid, but here the author includes even recommended acts as 'shurut' in the sense that they affect the perfection of prayer. The explanation clarifies that the four pillars (qiyam, qira'ah, ruku', sujud) are the only actual pillars; everything else, including takbir tahrimah and final sitting (as per some) and other acts, are conditions. This helps students organize the components of prayer into a hierarchy: pillars (arkan), conditions (shurut), and later wajibat and sunan will be detailed.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition required for the validity of prayer; can be external (before prayer) or internal (during prayer).
صحت (sihhat): Validity; an act conforming to Shariah requirements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the classification of elements like purity (taharah) and clean clothing?
A) They are pillars (arkan) of prayer.
B) They are conditions for the validity of beginning prayer.
C) They are conditions for the continuation of prayer.
D) They are nafl acts.

Key Takeaway
Prayer's non-pillar elements divide into external conditions (prerequisites for beginning) and internal conditions (requirements for continued validity).

The Four Pillars and Conditions of Prayer
Defines the four pillars of prayer and categorizes other requirements as conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four pillars of prayer according to Nur al-Idah and distinguish between pillars and conditions.

Core Idea
Prayer has only four pillars: standing, recitation, bowing, prostration—everything else is a condition.

This short passage from Nur al-Idah establishes the foundational building blocks of prayer. It clarifies that only four acts are pillars (arkan) that define the prayer itself. All other requirements are conditions (shurut), some external (before prayer) and some internal (during prayer). This distinction is crucial because omitting a pillar invalidates prayer, while violating a condition may or may not break it depending on the type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four pillars
From the text, list the four arkan: qiyam (standing), qira'ah (recitation), ruku' (bowing), sujud (prostration).
These are the components that define the prayer; omitting any one invalidates it.
2
Note the weaker opinion
The phrase 'wa qila' introduces a minority view that includes the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) as a pillar. Recognize that Nur al-Idah's position is the relied-upon view in this text, counting only four pillars.
This weaker opinion is mentioned but not adopted by the author.
3
Categorize the rest as conditions
The text says 'the rest are conditions'. Everything that is not one of the four pillars is a shart (condition).
This includes acts like takbir tahrimah, facing qibla, purity, etc., which are not pillars in this text.
4
Distinguish two types of conditions
Some conditions are for the validity of commencing prayer (e.g., wudu, facing qibla) and are external to the prayer. Others are for the continuity of its validity (e.g., remaining in a state of purity during prayer) and are internal.
This distinction affects when a condition must be fulfilled: before starting or throughout the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب) شروط الصلوة واركانها المذكورات اربعة القيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود . وقيل القعود الاخير مقدار التشهد ( ۳۷۱) وباقيها شرائط بعضها شرط لصحة الشروع في الصلوة وهو ما كان خارجها وغيره شرط لدوام صحتها .
Chapter: Conditions of prayer and its pillars. The mentioned (pillars) are four: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. And it is said: the final sitting (to the extent of tashahhud) (371). And the rest are conditions; some are conditions for the validity of commencing prayer, which are outside it, and others are conditions for the continuity of its validity.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah defines the pillars (arkan) and conditions (shurut) of prayer. The author states that only four actions are pillars: qiyam (standing), qira'ah (recitation), ruku' (bowing), and sujud (prostration). This differs from the Hidayah which counts six pillars including takbir tahrimah and the final sitting. The phrase 'wa qila' (and it is said) indicates a weaker opinion that considers the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) to be a pillar. The author clearly restricts pillars to four. Everything else is a condition: some are external (like purification, facing the qibla), required before starting prayer; others are internal (like maintaining purity of the body and place), required for the prayer to remain valid throughout. This distinction helps the student understand that the prayer's validity hinges on these four foundational acts, while the rest are preconditions or ongoing requirements.

Key Terms
اركان (arkan): Pillars of prayer; the essential acts without which prayer is invalid.
شرائط (shara'it): Conditions for the validity of prayer, both external and internal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Four: standing, recitation, bowing, prostration
C) Six: including takbir tahrimah and final sitting
D) Five: standing, recitation, bowing, prostration, final sitting

Key Takeaway
According to Nur al-Idah, prayer has four essential pillars; all other requirements are conditions, some applying before prayer and some during.

Praying on Impure Garments: Three Permissible Cases
Rulings on permissible prayer despite garment impurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where prayer is permissible on an impure garment and explain the rationale for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Prayer on a garment with impurity is valid when the impurity is on a thick underside or a separate, unattached lining.

In this lesson, we examine three specific exceptions to the general rule that prayer requires purity of garment. These exceptions are based on the thickness of material (libad), the separation of layers (non-stitched lining), and the worshipper's direct contact only with pure parts. Understanding these rulings helps you apply the principle that impurity is excused when it does not affect the worshipper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The general rule is that prayer requires purity of garment and body. Recognize that these are exceptions where the impurity is not considered to be on the worshipper's person.
2
List the three cases
Memorize the three cases: (1) thick felt rug with pure top and impure bottom, (2) pure garment with impure lining not sewn, (3) pure edge of a mat even if the impure side moves.
3
Extract the key factor for each
For each case, identify the factor that makes it permissible: thickness (ghilaz) in the felt rug; non-stitching (ghayr mudrib) for the lining; direct contact only with pure part for the edge.
4
Understand the rationale
The common rationale: the worshipper is not in direct contact with the impurity, and the impurity does not transfer to the person. The thickness, separation, and edge case all prevent the impurity from affecting the prayer.
5
Note the authority level
The phrase 'ala al-sahih indicates that this is the sound, relied-upon position in the Hanafi school. It is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : فصل في الثوب النجس (۳۷۲) تجوز الصلوة على لِبَدِ وجهه الأعلى طاهر والاسفل نجس وعلى ثوب طاهر وبطانته نجسة اذا كان غير مضرب وعلى طرف طاهر وان تحرّك الطرف النجس بحركته على الصحيح.
Chapter: On the impure garment. (372) Prayer is permissible on a felt rug whose upper side is pure and lower side is impure; and on a pure garment whose lining is impure if it is not stitched; and on a pure edge (of a mat/rug) even if the impure edge moves due to its movement, according to the sound (opinion).
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents three specific cases where prayer on or in contact with impurity is permissible. The first case: a thick felt rug (libad) with a pure top and impure bottom—prayer is valid because the thickness prevents the impurity from reaching the worshipper, and he is not carrying the impurity. The second case: a pure garment layered over an impure lining, provided the two are not stitched together (ghayr mudrib). If they are stitched, they count as one garment and the impurity spreads. The third case: a mat or rug with a pure edge; even if the impure part moves when the pure part is moved, prayer is valid because the worshipper's contact is only with the pure part. The phrase 'ala al-sahih (according to the sound opinion) indicates this is the relied-upon ruling in the school. The underlying principles include umum balwa (public need), thickness preventing transmission, and the worshipper not being a carrier of impurity.

Key Terms
لبِد (libad): A thick felt rug or mat that prevents impurity from seeping through.
بطانة (batinah): Lining of a garment; the inner layer that may be impure.
مضرب (mudrib): Sewn or stitched together; when two layers are sewn, they form one garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case is prayer permissible according to the passage?
A) A pure garment with an impure lining that is sewn to it
B) A felt rug with a pure top and impure bottom
C) Any garment with a small amount of impurity
D) A mat with an impure edge that does not move

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits prayer on garments where impurity is on a separate or non-contiguous part, based on thickness, separation, and direct contact.

Ruling on Prayer with a Partially Pure Cloth
Ruling on prayer when one finds cloth with at least one quarter pure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer when one has a cloth with a quarter pure and apply the rule regarding when it is wajib to wear it.

Core Idea
If one quarter of a cloth is pure, it is treated as entirely pure, making it wajib to wear for prayer.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling on a practical scenario: a person has only one cloth, and part of it is impure. The ruling hinges on the legal principle that one quarter is considered equivalent to the whole in certain contexts. Understanding this threshold is crucial for determining the obligation to cover the 'awrah during prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person has only one cloth, and it is partially pure and partially impure. He has no other option for covering his 'awrah.
2
Determine the threshold
Check whether the pure portion is one quarter or more, or less than one quarter.
The quarter is a key legal measure in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the legal principle
In many Hanafi rulings, one quarter is treated as the whole (e.g., wiping one quarter of the head suffices for the entire wiping in wudu). The same analogy applies here.
4
State the ruling
If the pure part is one quarter or more, it is wajib to wear the cloth; praying naked is not valid. If less than one quarter, the person has a choice, but wearing it is better.
The ruling balances covering the 'awrah and dealing with impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷۵) فان وجده ولو بالاباحة وربعه طاهر لا تـصــح صـلـوتـه عاريا وخير ان طهر اقل من ربعه (۳۷۵) اور اگر کپڑا پایا چاہے مباح کرنے کی وجہ سے ہو، اور اس کی چوتھائی پاک ہے تو ننگے ہو کر نماز جائز نہیں ہے ۔ اور اگر چوتھائی کپڑے سے کم پاک ہو تو تو اختیار دیا جائے گا۔ تشریح : بہت سے مقامات پر چوتھائی کو کل کے قائم مقام کیا ہے اس لئے یہاں بھی ایسا کپڑا املا کہ اس کی چوتھائی پاک ہے اور تین چوتھائی ناپاک ہے تو گویا کہ پورا پاک کپڑا پایا اس لئے اب نگا ہو کر نماز پڑھنا جائز نہیں اس کو پہن کر نماز پڑھے گا، چاہے وہ کپڑا مانگنے سے حاصل ہوتا ہو تب بھی مانگ کر پہنے۔ اور اگر چوتھائی کپڑے سے کم پاک ہو تو اب گویا کہ پاک نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کو اختیار ہوگا کہ کپڑا پہن کر نماز پڑھے، یانگا ہو کر نماز پڑھے، تاہم کپڑا پہن کر نماز پڑھنا زیادہ بہتر ہے۔
(375) If he finds a cloth, even if by permission, and one quarter of it is pure, his prayer naked is not valid. If less than one quarter is pure, he is given a choice. Explanation: In many places, one quarter is considered as the whole. So here, if a cloth has one quarter pure and three quarters impure, it is as if he has found a pure cloth; therefore praying naked is not allowed. He must wear it, even if he has to ask for it. If less than one quarter is pure, then it is as if not pure, so he has a choice: to pray wearing it or naked. However, wearing it is better.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This is a ruling about the obligation to cover the 'awrah when one has a cloth that is partially pure. The principle used is that one quarter of a thing is treated as the whole in many legal contexts (e.g., wiping one quarter of the head suffices for the whole in wudu). By analogy, if one has a cloth whose one quarter is pure, it is considered equivalent to having a completely pure cloth, so it becomes obligatory to wear it and not pray naked. If the pure part is less than one quarter, the cloth is not considered effectively pure, so the person has a choice: he may wear it (even though most of it is impure) or pray naked. The better option is to wear it to avoid exposure of 'awrah.

Key Terms
ربع طاهر (rub' tahir): One quarter of the cloth that is pure (free from najasah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may pray naked or wear it.
B) He must wear it and cannot pray naked.
C) He must pray naked because the cloth is mostly najis.
D) He must wash the cloth before wearing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when only one cloth is available, it is considered pure enough to cover the 'awrah if at least one quarter is pure, making it wajib to wear.

Praying in Completely Najis Cloth: Covering 'Awrah Over Purity
Ruling that praying in totally najis cloth is better than naked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying in a completely najis cloth and explain why covering the 'awrah is prioritized over the purity of the garment.

Core Idea
Covering the 'awrah takes priority over purity for the validity of prayer.

In the Hanafi school, when only an impure cloth is available for prayer, it is better to wear it than to pray naked. The text highlights that avoiding public exposure of the 'awrah takes precedence over the condition of purity. This lesson explores the reasoning and practical implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage addresses a situation where a person has only a completely najis (impure) cloth to wear for prayer. Determine the ruling in this case.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The text states that praying in the najis cloth is 'more beloved' (mustahabb) than praying naked. This is a recommendation, not an obligation.
The ruling is sunnah (recommended), not fard or wajib.
3
Analyze the reason
The commentary explains that praying naked may cause dislike among others due to exposure of the 'awrah. Thus, covering the 'awrah takes priority over the condition of the garment's purity.
This reflects a balance between satr (covering) and tahara (purity).
4
Identify the hukm level
The act of wearing the najis cloth for prayer is mustahabb (recommended). It is not obligatory, but it is better than the alternative.
If one prays naked out of necessity, the prayer is valid but less virtuous.
5
Note cross-madhhab perspective
Some other schools may prefer praying naked over using impure cloth. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf, but the Hanafi position prioritizes covering.
The Hanafi principle is that satr overrides the tahara requirement when no pure cloth is available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷۶) وصلوته في ثوب نجس الكل احب من عريان (۳۷۶) اور کل ناپاک کپڑے میں نماز پڑھنا زیادہ پسندیدہ ننگے نماز پڑھنے سے۔ تشریح : کل کپڑانا پاک ہے تب بھی بہتر یہ ہے کہ اس کپڑے کو پہن کر ہی نماز پڑھے، اس لئے نگا ہو کر نماز پڑھنے میں دوسروں کو بھی برا لگے گا۔
(376) And his prayer in a completely najis cloth is more beloved than (praying) naked. Explanation: Even if the cloth is completely najis, it is better to wear it for prayer because praying naked may cause dislike among others.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where the only cloth available is completely impure. The text states that praying in that cloth is preferable (mustahabb) to praying naked. The reason given in the commentary is that praying naked may cause others to perceive it as inappropriate or improper, even if the prayer itself would be valid due to necessity. The priority of covering the 'awrah takes precedence over the condition of purity when the alternative is exposure. This shows a balance between the requirements of purity and covering.

Key Terms
ثوب نجس الكل (thawb najis al-kull): A garment that is completely impure (najis) in all parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Covering Part of 'Awrah with Insufficient Cloth
Ruling on using cloth to cover only part of 'awrah when insufficient

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on covering only part of the 'awrah and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on which part to cover when insufficient cloth.

Core Idea
When cloth is insufficient for full 'awrah, covering at least part is wajib; opinions differ on prioritizing front or back.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a person in prayer has only a small cloth that cannot cover both front and back private parts. What must he do? The Hanafi school rules that using the cloth is obligatory, but which part to cover is contested among Hanafi scholars. Let's explore the ruling and the difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify that using the cloth to cover part of the 'awrah is wajib. If it can cover both front and back, both must be covered.
This is clear from the text.
2
Explore the ikhtilaf
When the cloth can only cover one part, there is disagreement: some say cover the back, some say cover the front. Recognize that both are valid opinions within the Hanafi school.
The text does not give preference.
3
Apply to a scenario
Consider a person with a small cloth that can only cover the front. According to one opinion, he must cover the front; according to the other, he should cover the back. Understand that either is acceptable.
The mufta bihi is not specified here; later works may give preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۷۷) وان وجد ما يستر بعض عورته وجب استعماله، ويستر القبل والدبر، وان لم يسع الا احدهما فقيل يستر الدبر وقيل القبل (۳۷۷) اور اگر اتنا کپڑا پایا کہ بعض عورت کو چھپایا جائے تو اس کا استعمال واجب ہے، اور قبل اور دبر کو چھپائے ، اور اگر ایک ہی چھپا سکے تو بعض حضرات نے فرمایا کہ دبر کو چھپائے ، اور بعض نے فرمایا کہ قبل کو چھپائے۔
(377) If he finds cloth that can cover part of his 'awrah, its use is obligatory, and he covers the front and back. If it can only cover one, some say he covers the back, and some say he covers the front.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where one has only a small cloth that can cover only part of the 'awrah (the area between navel and knee). The ruling states that it is obligatory (wajib) to use such a cloth. Ideally, both the front (qabl) and back (dubur) should be covered. If the cloth is insufficient to cover both, there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: some say to cover the back because it is less likely to be exposed accidentally, and some say to cover the front because it is considered more private. The text does not give a tarjih (preference), so both views are valid within the madhhab. The practical consequence is that one should cover as much as possible.

Key Terms
قبل و دبر (qabl wa dubur): The front private part (genitals) and the back private part (anus).

Key Terms from Source Passage
